Project 6: Advanced Text Analysis with SpaCy¶

Overview¶

In this project, we will enhance our text analysis techniques by applying proper data pre-processing to extract more meaningful information from literary texts. * Choose another text that interests you (a novel, article, speech, etc.) (You should make another copy of this colab file and change the name to CS110_Project6_Part2.ipynb)

  • Produce 10 to 15 most frequrently used words without text preprocessing
  • Apply the same pre-processing steps using SpaCy
  • Identify the 15 most frequently used meaningful words
  • Write a 1-2 paragraph analysis of what these words reveal about the text's content and themes, and why we need text pre-processing now that you have worked on at least two sets of text

Project Objectives¶

  • Apply proper text pre-processing techniques to extract meaningful words

  • Use SpaCy to clean and normalize text data

  • Identify the 15 most frequently used meaningful words in a text

  • Analyze how pre-processing affects text analysis results

  • Apply the same techniques to a text of your choice

Part 0: Text analysis without text cleaning (Lab6)¶

Loading Data¶

We will fetch the first two chapters of Dracula from Project Gutenberg

Function to fetch data¶

In [ ]:
def fetch_text(raw_url):
  import requests
  from pathlib import Path
  import hashlib

  CACHE_DIR = Path("cs_110_content/text_cache")
  CACHE_DIR.mkdir(parents=True, exist_ok=True)

  def _url_to_filename(url):
    url_hash = hashlib.sha1(url.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest()[:12]
    return CACHE_DIR / f"{url_hash}.txt"

  cache_path = _url_to_filename(raw_url)

  SUCCESS_MSG = "✅ Text fetched."
  FAILURE_MSG = "❌ Failed to fetch text."
  try:
    if not cache_path.exists():
      response = requests.get(raw_url, timeout=10)
      response.raise_for_status()
      text_data = response.text
      cache_path.write_text(text_data, encoding="utf-8")
    print(SUCCESS_MSG)
    return cache_path.read_text(encoding="utf-8")

  except Exception as e:
    print(FAILURE_MSG)
    print(f"Error: {e}")
    return ""

Save the text in a variable¶

In [ ]:
DRACULA_URL = "https://www.gutenberg.org/cache/epub/345/pg345.txt"
dracula_text = fetch_text(DRACULA_URL)

✅ Text fetched.

Statistics about the data

In [ ]:
def print_text_stats(text):
  num_chars = len(text)

  lines = text.splitlines()
  num_lines = len(lines)

  num_words = 0
  for line in lines:
    words_in_line = line.split()
    num_words_in_line = len(words_in_line)
    num_words += num_words_in_line

  print(f"Number of characters: {num_chars}")
  print(f"Number of lines: {num_lines}")
  print(f"Number of words: {num_words}")

print_text_stats(dracula_text)

Number of characters: 865173
Number of lines: 15851
Number of words: 164351
In [ ]:
def get_word_counts(text):
  word_counts = {}
  lines = text.splitlines()
  for line in lines:
    words = line.split()
    for word in words:
      word = word.lower()
      if word in word_counts:
        word_counts[word] += 1
      else:
        word_counts[word] = 1
  return word_counts

word_counts = get_word_counts(dracula_text)
print(word_counts)

In [ ]:
# prompt: Make a new Code Cell below and use Gemini to create a new function called print_top_10_frequent_words that will call the above get_word_counts() and print only the top 10 frequent words.

import operator

def print_top_10_frequent_words(text):
    word_counts = get_word_counts(text)
    sorted_word_counts = dict(sorted(word_counts.items(), key=operator.itemgetter(1), reverse=True))
    top_10_words = list(sorted_word_counts.items())[:10]  # Get the top 10 words and counts
    for word, count in top_10_words:
        print(f"{word}: {count}")

print_top_10_frequent_words(dracula_text)

Part 1: Analyzing "Jane Eyre" with Pre-processing¶

In our previous lab, we simply counted word frequencies without any sophisticated pre-processing, resulting in common but uninformative words (like "the", "and", "to") dominating our results.

You will use SpaCy, a powerful Natural Language Processing library, to perform these operations. SpaCy provides pre-trained models that can handle tokenization, stop word removal, and lemmatization automatically.

In [ ]:
# install spacy library
#!pip install spacy (uncomment it to install)
In [ ]:
# bring the spacy library into scope
import spacy
In [ ]:
# Load a SpaCy model
nlp = spacy.load('en_core_web_sm')

Explanation:

spacy: This is the spaCy library, a popular and efficient NLP library in Python.

load(): This function loads a pre-trained NLP model.

'en_core_web_sm': This is the name of the small English-language model. It's a lightweight model that includes:

  • Tokenization (splitting text into words, punctuation, etc.)

  • Part-of-speech (POS) tagging

  • Named entity recognition (NER) etc

nlp: This variable now holds the loaded model.

In [ ]:
def word_tokenization_normalization(text):

    text = text.lower() # lowercase
    doc = nlp(text)     # loading text into model

    words_normalized = []
    for word in doc:
        if word.text != '\n' \
        and not word.is_stop \
        and not word.is_punct \
        and not word.like_num \
        and len(word.text.strip()) > 2:
            word_lemmatized = str(word.lemma_)
            words_normalized.append(word_lemmatized)

    return words_normalized

  1. Use "Explain Code" AI feature to understand what this function, word_tokenization_normalization(text) does and write a short summary of about what it does. You may use bullet points. (Please create another text cell under this cell to write the summary.)

The above function cleans and normalizes a givin text through performing multiple pre-processing steps. This allows for a more meaningful analysis since it removes words that are not informative or redundant. Some of its functions include converting letters to lowercase, removing insignifigant words and punctuation marks, and analyzing the base forms of words.

# prompt: Help me create a new function called create_word_frequency_dict from the resulting output of the previous code that returns a word frequency dictionary, then call this function with the text Pride and Prejudice

def create_word_frequency_dict(normalized_words):
    word_frequency = {}
    for word in normalized_words:
        if word in word_frequency:
            word_frequency[word] += 1
        else:
            word_frequency[word] = 1
    return word_frequency

# prompt: Create a new function called print_top_words that print out the most frequently used words of a text, then call this function to find the frequency of the top words in the text Pride and Prejudice

import operator

def print_top_words(text, top_n=15):
    """Prints the top N most frequent words in the given text."""

    normalized_words = word_tokenization_normalization(text)
    word_freq = create_word_frequency_dict(normalized_words)
    sorted_word_counts = dict(sorted(word_freq.items(), key=operator.itemgetter(1), reverse=True))

    top_words = list(sorted_word_counts.items())[:top_n]
    for word, count in top_words:
        print(f"{word}: {count}")

print_top_words(dracula_text)

For my text, Dracula, I analyzed the enitre text which I think may be the reason why my top 15 meaningful words seem less signifigant. Even so, it creates a much better idea of the text's true meaning. From the words I assume that the something or someone is coming somewhere and information is being gathered over it, thus resulting in words like know, say, look, time, tell, think, and so on. From pre-processing both this text and Pride and Prejudice I have noticed that it is a quick way to get a general idea of a text. Without it, as seen in the wordlist before the pre-processing, it is very hard to make out the text's content the analysis results in mostly prepositions or articles. When we remove these non-informative words the true meaning of the text is more clear, showing that text pre-processing is indeed a useful function.
